<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e2051ead489e480f80291aca68e8bc9d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ff3555a7-6e4e-4aed-9ac2-e108762d1ea5'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d136765738" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Biphasic recombination is an effective strategy to boost the electrochemical properties of sodium layered oxides. Here, we deploy a controlled ratio of P2 structural intergrowth in the manganese-enriched O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> model compound with the overall composition maintained, enabling a high energy density to be achieved.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/df95e5c9-185c-4419-9cd2-f7c30e8eb60a/anie202411059-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/df95e5c9-185c-4419-9cd2-f7c30e8eb60a/anie202411059-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aae5ee94-1d3f-4f10-b5ad-71363b37a0b5/anie202411059-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/df95e5c9-185c-4419-9cd2-f7c30e8eb60a/anie202411059-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d136765887" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Anionic redox chemistry can surpass theoretical limits of conventional layered oxide cathodes in energy density. A recent model system of sodium-ion batteries, O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, demonstrated full anionic redox capacity but is limited in reversibility and kinetics due to irreversible structural rearrangement and oxygen loss. Solutions to these issues are missing due to the challenging synthesis. Here, we harness the unique structural richness of sodium layered oxides and realize a controlled ratio of P2 structural intergrowth in this model compound with the overall composition maintained. The resulted O3 with 27 % P2 intergrowth structure delivers an excellent initial Coulombic efficiency of 87 %, comparable to the state-of-the-art Li-rich NMCs. This improvement is attributed to the effective suppression of irreversible oxygen release and structural changes, evidenced by operando Differential Electrochemical Mass Spectroscopy and X-ray Diffraction. The as-prepared intergrowth material, based on the environmentally benign Mn, exhibits a reversible capacity of 226 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at C/20 rate with excellent cycling stability stemming from the redox reactions of oxygen and manganese. Our work isolates the role of P2 structural intergrowth and thereby introduces a novel strategy to enhance the reversibility and kinetics of anionic redox reactions in sodium layered cathodes without compromising capacity.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411059-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411059-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Future large-scale energy storage requires highly efficient yet low-cost battery technologies, for which sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a major candidate.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0001_R_d136764921e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Among the choices of cathode materials, which are the bottleneck for the energy density and cost of SIBs, layered oxides (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, 0 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1, TM = transition metals) are most investigated due to their high theoretical capacity, flexible composition and versatile structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0004_R_d136764921e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Moreover, layered oxides could host anionic redox that can further access excess capacity beyond the theoretical limit of transition metal redox, as demonstrated in lithium-ion batteries with Li-rich layered oxides.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0007_R_d136764921e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> The Na-rich counterparts, however, only exist as model systems with 4<i>d</i> or 5<i>d</i> elements such as Ru, Ir, etc.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0012_R_d136764921e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> On the other hand, anionic redox with 3<i>d</i> metals was first realized in Na deficient, P2-type layered oxides by introducing Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup> or vacancy.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0017_R_d136764921e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> However, the full potential of anionic redox can only be unlocked in O3-type 3<i>d</i> metal oxides having unity Na content, for which the synthesis has been a long-standing challenge until O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was first achieved by Tarascon et al.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> This O3 manganese oxide with full Na content can deliver ~250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity in the first charge solely from anionic redox. Moreover, the material has many positive attributes such as no voltage fade. However, the exclusive oxygen redox for such high capacity inevitably leads to structural rearrangement and irreversible oxygen release. Combining the capacity benefit with fast kinetics and good reversibility remains a fundamental challenge.</p>
         
         <p>Unlike their lithium counterparts, sodium layered oxides have unique and fascinating structural richness that can be harnessed to improve the kinetics and reversibility. While high-energy-density O3 phases (up to <i>x</i> = 1) often suffer from poor capacity retention and limited rate performance,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0023_R_d136764921e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Mater. Chem. A">7</a></sup></span> Na-deficient P2 phases (typically 0.6 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 0.83) have complementary benefits.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0024_R_d136764921e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> In particular, P-type structure seems to better stabilize anionic redox, as reported in P3-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> where the Na occupying prismatic sites can inhibit Mn migration and better tolerate lattice distortion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0025_R_d136764921e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Joule">9</a></sup></span> In P2/O3 biphasic Na<sub>0.67</sub>Li<sub>0.11</sub>Fe<sub>0.36</sub>Mn<sub>0.36</sub>Ti<sub>0.17</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, P2-type structure has also been found beneficial to suppress oxygen release.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0026_R_d136764921e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Despite the prospects of combing the merits, the beneficial role of structural intergrowth in anionic-redox-based compounds is still not conclusive. This is because previous attempts on O3/P2 intergrowth structure with anionic redox convolves structural intergrowth with compositional factors, as they all had to change the TM composition and/or Na content to tune the O or P phase.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0027_R_d136764921e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> In particular, changes in compositions also result in different participation levels of cationic redox reactions, which would interfere with anionic redox behaviors.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0030_R_d136764921e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem.">12</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Here, we realize controlled P2-type intergrowth into the O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> model compound while keeping the overall composition nearly unchanged, thereby isolating the effects of pure structural modification on anionic redox behaviors. This is possible by carefully tuning the synthesis conditions to exploit the boundary of O3 and P2 single-phase domains in the framework of cationic potential described by Hu et al.,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0031_R_d136764921e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Science">13</a></sup></span> where minor variations in the composition by synthesis control will be sufficient to tune the O3 and P2 phase without chemical substitution. The resulted phase with 27 % P2 intergrowth possesses ~1 Na and delivers a high capacity of 260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first charge solely coming from anionic redox but with much suppressed oxygen release as demonstrated by differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS). Consequently, the phase yields an excellent reversibility with a discharge capacity as high as 226 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a first-cycle Coulombic efficiency of 87 %. Besides, the novel intergrowth material also exhibits excellent rate capability and delivers 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1C rate, which can be retained 84.37 % after 100 cycles. The charge compensation mechanism and structural evolution for the excellent electrochemical properties are revealed by a combination of spectroscopy tools and operando X-ray diffraction.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411059-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411059-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202411059-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202411059-sec-0003-title"> Structural Properties</h3>
            
            <p>The O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compound could not be synthesized phase pure despite various attempts,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0031_R_d136764921e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Science">13</a>, <a href="#anie202411059-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0032_R_d136764921e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span> until Tarascon et al. introduced a new set of precursors under controlled atmosphere.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> Following this route, we targeted distinct O3/P2 intergrowth ratios with the same overall NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> composition by carefully adjusting the duration of argon gas flushing while heating the same mixture of stoichiometric amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Li<sub>2</sub>O and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> precursors (see experimental procedures in Supporting Information for more details). In addition to using a consistent amount of Na<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, we maintained the same sintering temperature (700 °C), duration, heating and cooling rates to ensure constant Na content and O<sub>2</sub> source. As Na<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> releases O<sub>2</sub> upon heating, controlling the argon flushing duration therefore fine tunes the balance between oxidizing and reducing local atmosphere. The X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) patterns of the as-synthesized materials show characteristic peaks of both P2 (<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> space group) and O3 structures with reflections from superstructure ordering (20–23° 2<i>θ</i>) that can be taken into account by <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> space group (Figure S1). The pure O3 phase was obtained with an Ar flushing duration (<i>t</i>) of 150 min. Interestingly, deviations from this duration in either direction leads to the formation of a P2 second phase. Fine screening of the Ar flushing duration close to <i>t</i>=150 min (Figure S1c–d) confirms the non-monotonous behavior of the P2 phase growth, with the refined P2 phase ratios increasing as the deviation from the O3-pure-phase condition increased. This observation aligns with a DFT study predicting that the fully sodiated O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is thermodynamically less stable than the Na-deficient P2 phases and therefore very difficult to synthesize.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0032_R_d136764921e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span> We believe that the pure O3 phase was stabilized thanks to the balance between Ar and O<sub>2</sub>, whereas deviations from this balance result in P2 phases that are thermodynamically more stable.</p>
            
            <p>While O3/P2 intergrowth compounds can be obtained with no or minimal Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub> impurity (Figure S1-4) with Ar flushing time between 120 and 210 min, larger deviations in both directions (&lt; 120 min, more oxidizing; and &gt; 210 min, more reducing) result in impurities such as Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub> and NaMnO<sub>2</sub> (Figure S1–2). We selected three novel O3/P2 biphasic materials with no or minimal Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub> impurity (Figure S4) with Ar flushing time between 120 and 210 min. Their XRD patterns were Rietveld refined using the <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> space group for the O3 phase and the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> space group for the P2 phase (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0001">1a</a>–<a href="#anie202411059-fig-0001">d</a>, Table S1, S3, S5, S7). We further validated the O3/P2 phase ratios by refining the same XRD patterns using the <i>R</i>-3<i>m</i> space group for the O3 phase and the same <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> space group, which confirmed similar ratios (Table S2, S4, S6, S8). The average O3/P2 phase ratio were quantified by Rietveld refinements as 87 : 13, 80 : 20, and 73 : 27, respectively (Table S9). The refinement also revealed <i>d</i>-spacings of O3 and P2 phases to be ~5.3 Å and ~5.5 Å in all samples. We will refer to the three biphasic materials as O3/13P2, O3/20P2, O3/27P2 hereafter. Even for the material with the highest P2 ratio, O3/27P2, the composition was confirmed to be Na<sub>0.97</sub>Li<sub>0.29</sub>Mn<sub>0.71</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES), consistent with our Rietveld refinement results that revealed 27 % of P2-Na<sub>0.76</sub>(Li,Mn)O<sub>2</sub> and 73 % of O3-Na<sub>1</sub>(Li,Mn)O<sub>2</sub>. High angle annular dark field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) images on the surface of the particle revealed clear columns of Mn atoms similar to the those in the bulk, with no clear sign of Mn vacancy or enrichment (Figure S5). Although we cannot fully exclude the possibility of having Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> residues and defects on the surface,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0033_R_d136764921e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Power Sources">15</a></sup></span> the ICP and refinement results validate our successful control of the O3/P2 ratio by tuning the synthesis atmosphere without significantly modifying the overall Na stoichiometry or transition metal composition. To rationalize it, we turned to the concept of cationic potential by Hu et al.,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0031_R_d136764921e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Science">13</a></sup></span> widely accepted as a predictor for O3 or P2 single-phase preference of a given composition. The O3/P2 composition deduced from ICP-OES and Rietveld refinement results falls close to the dividing line of O3 and P2 single-phase regions (Figure S6), suggesting the possibility of fine-tuning the structural ratio with minor variations in the composition. To enhance the comparison, P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2) with purely Mn<sup>4+</sup> was synthesized by solid state-method as the control group and the XRD pattern is shown in Figure S7.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202411059-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/af60388e-6a67-4b87-9338-19cbb4b08a73/anie202411059-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/af60388e-6a67-4b87-9338-19cbb4b08a73/anie202411059-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d22f12a3-228d-4530-b2b6-0f7be0144380/anie202411059-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/af60388e-6a67-4b87-9338-19cbb4b08a73/anie202411059-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411059-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411059" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structure characterizations of pristine O3/P2-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials. (a–d) Rietveld refinement of the XRD patterns for four NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials with varying intergrowth ratios obtained through different durations of argon flush during synthesis (<i>t</i>). (e) Schematic O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> structures. (f) SEM image, (g) HRTEM image (FFT image insert) and (h–i) enlarged views of P2 and O3 regions.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images on the O3/P2 biphasic material show flake morphology, with particle sizes of ~1 μm, similar to the pure O3 phase (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0001">1f</a>, Figure S8). Next, we used high resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) to elucidate the spatial distribution of O3 and P2 phases on the nanoscale. HRTEM images of the O3/27P2 material reveal nano-domains with interlayer distances of ~5.51 Å and ~5.32 Å (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0001">1g</a>–<a href="#anie202411059-fig-0001">i</a>), which are consistent with the XRD refinement results and correspond to the (002) planes of the P2 structure and the (003) planes of the O3 structure, respectively. The fast Fourier transform (FFT) image of selected area also displays diffraction spots of the (100) plane of P2 structure in addition to the (006), (101), and (012) planes of O3 structure (Figure S9).</p>
            
            <p>These results confirm that the two phases coexist in the same crystallite and an atomic scale intergrowth in the newly synthesized material. In addition, the energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elementary mapping reveals the uniform distribution of Na, Mn, and O across the crystallite (Figure S10).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202411059-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202411059-sec-0004-title"> Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical properties of the three O3/P2 intergrowth materials were assessed in Na half cells to compare with the O3 pure phase. Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0002">2a</a>–<a href="#anie202411059-fig-0002">b</a> shows the voltage profiles and the corresponding derivative plots of the four cathode materials between 4.5 and 1.5 V at C/20 rate. The voltage profiles of intergrowth structures resemble that of the pure O3 phase, exhibiting a long voltage plateau around 3.6 V upon the first charge, corresponding to a sharp oxidation peak in the derivative plot. An additional oxidation peak at approximately 4.2 V in the derivative plot becomes more pronounced with an increasing P2 ratio. This phenomenon might be associated with anionic redox reactions in the P2 phase, given that the voltage aligns with typical values for anionic redox reactions in P2 single-phase materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0034_R_d136764921e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span> as will be examined later with spectroscopy techniques. The initial discharge curves of all materials exhibit an S-shaped curve that persists upon extended cycling, indicating an electrochemical desodiation induced structural transformation during the first charge, as will be discussed later. Interestingly, an improvement in first-cycle Coulombic efficiency is observed with an increasing P2 ratio, steadily from 79 % (pure O3) to 87 % (O3/27P2). Notably, the O3/27P2 compound achieves the highest reversible capacity of 226 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, surpassing that of high-capacity O3 sodium layered oxides. To examine the role of extra sodiation into the P2 component to this higher discharge capacity compared to that of the pure O3 phase, we compared both the O3/27P2 and O3 phase starting directly on discharge (Figure S11). Both phases show negligible sodiation capacities of 12 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. This difference cannot fully account for the superior capacity of the O3/27P2 intergrowth, therefore pointing to a higher reversibility of the processes involved in the initial cycle.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202411059-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/88b4f8ef-46c1-4190-a0d8-7bffac313e87/anie202411059-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/88b4f8ef-46c1-4190-a0d8-7bffac313e87/anie202411059-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/06576be1-d3d4-4015-914a-29e77ff90a1b/anie202411059-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/88b4f8ef-46c1-4190-a0d8-7bffac313e87/anie202411059-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411059-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411059" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance of O3/P2-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes with different intergrowth-ratios. (a) First three cycles of galvanostatic charge/discharge within 1.5–4.5 V range at a current rate of C/20, with their first discharge capacity and first-cycle Coulombic efficiency being noted. (b) Corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots. (c) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the second cycle at C/20, C/10, C/5, C/2 and 1C, after an activation cycle at C/20.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Given the observed “activation” process in the first cycle, we examined the rate capability of the explored series in the subsequent cycle after a common activation cycle at C/20 (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0002">2c</a>). The intergrowth materials demonstrate enhanced rate capabilities for both charge and discharge compared to the O3 pure phase, in line with previous reports highlighting the beneficial role of intergrowth.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0035_R_d136764921e1022" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span> Overall, the O3/27P2 compound exhibits the largest capacity, the highest first-cycle Coulombic efficiency and the best rate performance. Consequently, we will focus on this compound with the highest P2 ratio for thorough comparison with O3 and P2 pure phases, aiming to unravel the underlying mechanism for the improvement. Next, we compare the O3/27P2 to the O3-type NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P2-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compounds, where manganese exclusively exists in the Mn<sup>4+</sup> state. These two compounds are chosen as controls since their charge compensation mechanism in the first electrochemical charging process has been established to rely solely on oxygen oxidation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#anie202411059-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0053_R_d136764921e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span> Similar to the O3 and P2 pure phases, the O3/27P2 compound exhibits long charge plateau only in the first cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">3a</a>–<a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">c</a>, Figure S12). To gain a closer insight into the redox couples at play, we performed cyclic voltammetry on all three samples at a scan rate of 0.1 mV/s within the voltage range of 1.5–4.5 V (Figure S13). In the O3 phase, the major anodic peak appears at around 4.0 V upon the first positive polarization, while in the P2 phase, a major peak is observed at 4.4 V. Notably, the biphasic O3/27P2 compound exhibits two significant anodic peaks, one at 4.1 V and the other at 4.4 V, suggesting a combination of processes reminiscent of the oxygen oxidation mechanisms observed in both the O3 and P2 single-phase structures.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202411059-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d199631a-5a75-4763-b579-dd1969448528/anie202411059-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d199631a-5a75-4763-b579-dd1969448528/anie202411059-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b46f08d8-715c-4c05-95c9-bee64c30f98e/anie202411059-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d199631a-5a75-4763-b579-dd1969448528/anie202411059-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411059-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411059" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical properties of O3/27P2 cathode in comparison to pure O3- and P2-type cathodes. (a–c) The first four cycles of galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of O3/27P2-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, respectively. (d) Cycling performance at increasing current rates from C/20 to 1C of the three compounds. (e) Capacity retention of the three compounds at the charge–discharge rate of 1C. (f) Overall performance of the three compounds for practical application. (g–h) The best-performing O3/27P2 with respect to state-of-the-art Na-ion cathodes, in terms of (g) reversible capacity, average discharge voltage, and energy density available half-cell data at C/10 rate from reference;<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0024" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0024_R_d136764921e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202411059-bib-0036" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0036_R_d136764921e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> and (h) Ragone plot including representatives from three leading high-rate cathode material types to date, namely the O/P biphasic structures, high-entropy layered oxides and polyanionic compounds.<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0043" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0043_R_d136764921e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18g - Sci Bull (Beijing)">18g</a>, <a href="#anie202411059-bib-0047" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0047_R_d136764921e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span></p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>In literature, P2/O3 intergrowth structures are often reported as having better rate capability and cycling stability over pure phases due to synergy between phases.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0054_R_d136764921e1106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span> We wonder where our biphasic material having only 27 % of P2 phase stands in terms of rate capability compared to the pure P2 phase having anionic redox. Rate capability was tested at increasing current rates of C/20 to 1C for 5 consecutive cycles at each rate (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">3d</a>). Notably, the biphasic compound exhibited superior capacity compared to the two pure phases at every tested rate. Even when charged and discharged at 1C (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">3d</a>), the biphasic cathode provides a decent reversible capacity of 132 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, equivalent to 60 % of its capacity at C/20 and 75 % of its capacity at C/10, largely surpassing that of the O3 and P2 compounds (76.2 and 112.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively). The enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics over the O3 pure phase is also evidenced by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) in Figure S14.</p>
            
            <p>Given the high rate capability of the O3/27P2 phase, we decided to test its adaptivity to high rate without activation cycles at smaller rates. The O3/27P2 phase shows a more pronounced advantage in high-rate adaptivity and stable high capacity when directly charged and discharged at 1C (285 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">3e</a> and Figure S15). All three materials take several cycles to fully activate before reaching peak capacity. The O3/27P2 and the pure P2 compounds activate quickly within 5 cycles and reach ~150 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and ~110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, while the capacity of the O3 phase stabilizes gradually over 20 cycles to a smaller capacity of ~108 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Over 100 cycles at 1C rate, the O3/27P2 shows a capacity retention of 84.37 % after 100 cycles, far exceeding 55.46 % of the P2 phase. Furthermore, we compare its discharge capacity at a 1C rate with state-of-the-art Mn-based layered oxide cathodes in O3 and P2 single- or bi-phasic materials (Table S12). The newly synthesized O3/27P2 showcases the highest accessible capacity at 1C with the most stable retention.</p>
            
            <p>The relevant electrochemical metrics are united in the spider chart in Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">3f</a>. The O3/27P2 intergrowth structure outperforms pure P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> phases across almost every aspect. It also compares favorably to state-of-the-art layered oxide cathodes crystallized either in P2, O3 single phases or biphasic structures summarized in Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">3g</a>. In addition, we use the Ragone plot (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0003">3h</a>) to reveal the superior energy densities of our novel biphasic cathode over a wide power density range, with respect to representatives from the three leading high-rate cathode material types to date: O3/P2 biphasic oxides, high-entropy oxides, and polyanionic compounds.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202411059-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202411059-sec-0005-title"> Charge Compensation Mechanism and Gas Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>Next, we studied the charge compensation mechanism in O3/27P2 via combined ex situ characterization techniques including X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (XPS), X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), and electron paramagnetic resonance spectroscopy (EPR).</p>
            
            <p>The XPS spectra of Mn 2<i>p</i>, Mn 3 <i>s</i> and O 1<i>s</i> XPS (Figure S16) on the pristine sample confirms the oxidation state of Mn<sup>4+</sup> (binding energy, BE = 642 eV) and O<sup>2−</sup> (BE = 529.5 eV) in the as-synthesized O3/27P2 material. Upon the first charge, a new peak at BE ~530.5 eV gradually intensifies in the O 1s spectra and is assigned to the oxidized lattice oxygen O<sup>n−</sup> (n&lt;2) in light of previous reports.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0058_R_d136764921e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, no change in the Mn 2<i>p</i> spectra can be detected, indicating the exclusive contribution of anionic redox during the initial charge. During the first discharge, O<sup>n−</sup> species are mainly reduced with however the appearance at 1.5 V of a new shoulder on the lower binding energy side of the Mn<sup>4+</sup> peak that corresponds to Mn<sup>3+</sup>. At the same time, the energy separation between the two peaks of Mn 3<i>s</i> core level spectrum increases, confirming the decrease of Mn valance state at the end of discharge.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0062_R_d136764921e1201" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Catal.">23</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>It is well known that the energy position and multiplet spectral feature at the Mn-<i>L</i><sub>2,3</sub> edge are highly sensitive to the valence state,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0063_R_d136764921e1209" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> and local environment of Mn ions,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0067_R_d136764921e1212" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span> respectively. Soft XAS at Mn <i>L</i>-edge (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0004">4a</a>) performed in total electron yield (TEY) mode also confirms that the Mn<sup>4+</sup> ion is not oxidized during first charge to 4.5 V (blue line), but is clearly reduced during the discharge to 1.5 V (green line). Thus, we expect that upon charge, the further oxidization occurs at oxygen, which can be observed in the O-<i>K</i> XAS spectra. Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0004">4b</a> shows the soft XAS spectra of the charged and discharged samples at the O <i>K</i>-edge taken with total fluorescence yield (TFY) mode, which offers greater bulk sensitivity with a probe depth of ~100 nm. The pre-edge peaks below 533 eV are widely acknowledged as transitions from the O 1<i>s</i> to O 2<i>p</i> states, which are mixed with empty Mn 3<i>d</i> states.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0071_R_d136764921e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Alloys Compd.">26</a></sup></span> For pristine Mn<sup>4+</sup> state, the peak A at ~529.5 eV is assigned to unoccupied <i>e</i><sub>g</sub><sup>↑</sup>/<i>t</i><sub><i>2</i>g</sub><sup>↓</sup> states, while the peak B at ~532 eV is attributed to unoccupied <i>e</i><sub>g</sub><sup>↓</sup> state.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0072_R_d136764921e1257" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Electron Spectrosc.">27</a></sup></span> The significantly increased intensity of the lower energy peaks upon charge (blue) as compared with pristine sample (black), in light of the unvaried manganese oxidation state, is undoubtedly relevant to electron removal from oxygen 2<i>p</i> states, since an increase in the valence of 3<i>d</i> metal by one would lead to a shift of the pre-edge peak by ca. 1 eV.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0073_R_d136764921e1265" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28">28</a></sup></span> In Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0004">4b</a>, the O-<i>K</i> XAS spectra also exhibited the decrease in the intensity of the two pre-edge peaks during discharge (green) indicating a decrease in Mn valence as found in the Mn-<i>L</i><sub>2,3</sub> edge. This affirms only O oxidation, but not Mn during charge and subsequent Mn reduction during discharge within the bulk of the O3/27P2 material. The extra intensity at 533.5 eV in the discharged sample can be attributed to surface carbonates from electrolyte decomposition,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0076_R_d136764921e1277" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Energy">29</a></sup></span> which is likely reoxidized during the second charge.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202411059-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4dc1051b-feac-4b60-9fb2-e1ce2bf6070a/anie202411059-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4dc1051b-feac-4b60-9fb2-e1ce2bf6070a/anie202411059-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/90b4f36b-ad95-4077-bc78-2cf3984eb207/anie202411059-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4dc1051b-feac-4b60-9fb2-e1ce2bf6070a/anie202411059-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411059-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411059" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Charge compensation mechanism of Mn and O; Gas evolution of O3/27P2 and O3 upon cycling. (a) Mn <i>L</i>-edge sXAS spectra under TEY mode at various charge and discharge states. The spectra of reference Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub>, Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, and MnO were also shown for comparison. (b) Ex situ O <i>K</i>-edge sXAS spectra under TFY mode. (c) The X-band EPR spectra of pristine and ex situ O3/27P2 cathodes. EPR measurements were performed at 90 K. The operando DEMS results of gas evolution during the first cycle at C/10 of (d) O3/27P2 and (e) O3.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Lastly, to fully probe the bulk of the sample, we carried out ex situ EPR (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0004">4c</a>) that is sensitive to unpaired or delocalized electron spins.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0077_R_d136764921e1323" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span> The pristine sample shows a broad symmetric signal centered at g ≈ 2.00, corresponding to antiferromagnetic Mn<sup>4+</sup> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/723415fb-7ddc-4991-88c8-cf7f37ff7a31/anie202411059-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
, <i>S</i> = 3/2).<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0078" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0078_R_d136764921e1358" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span> The sharp signal at g value at around 2 is from the conductive carbon black (grey bar) for the cycled electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0082_R_d136764921e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span> Upon charge, there is a broadening of the Mn<sup>4+</sup> signal and the appearance of a new signal with much narrower line shape centered at g ≈ 2.007 (marked by pink ellipse). This newly identified signal can be assigned as (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup> according to previous reports.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0083" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0083_R_d136764921e1370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span> And the gradual broadening of the Mn<sup>4+</sup> signal is supposed to be a manifestation of the enhanced paramagnetism of Mn<sup>4+</sup> under the influence of oxidation oxygen.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0087" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0087_R_d136764921e1378" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Storage Mater.">34</a></sup></span> During discharge, the line shape of (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup> loses its intensity while the line shape of pristine O3/27P2 slightly recovers suggesting the decrease of (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup>. However, in the sample discharged to 1.5 V, there is a prominent intensity decrease compared to the pristine sample, suggesting the reduction of Mn<sup>4+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bf1ef805-37f6-49fb-8828-5af7e550e91f/anie202411059-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
) that is EPR-silent under conventional mode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0088" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0088_R_d136764921e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Chem. Soc. Chem. Commun.">35</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>Altogether, our results unambiguously confirmed that the initial charge capacity of the O3/27P2 material is exclusively attributed to the bulk oxygen oxidation, while Mn<sup>4+</sup> being redox inactive. On the other hand, all spectroscopic techniques collectively demonstrate that the reduction of oxidized oxygen and Mn<sup>4+</sup> during the initial discharge. Furthermore, the spectra of second end-of-charge sample resemble those of the first end-of-charge in every technique, confirming the reversibility of the anionic redox process after the initial charge.</p>
            
            <p>Based on prior research, the O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compound, with its first charge capacity (250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, ~0.9 Na<sup>+</sup> removal) solely compensated by oxygen oxidation, inevitably suffers from oxygen release and hence irreversibility.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> In contrast, the P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compound achieves its initial charge compensation through reversible anionic redox without oxygen release.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0089" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0089_R_d136764921e1455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Mater.">36</a></sup></span> We therefore decided to monitor gas evolution during the first cycle of O3/27P2 in a Na half cell by operando differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS). Surprisingly, the O3/27P2 electrode exhibits no discernible O<sub>2</sub> evolution during the first charge–discharge cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0004">4d</a>), in contrast to the evident O<sub>2</sub> degassing toward the end of first charge (onset around 4 V) in the pure O3 phase under identical experimental conditions (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0004">4e</a>). In fact, the oxygen release in the O3 material is accompanied by an even higher flux of CO<sub>2</sub> evolution in the Na half cell, which is likely due to immediate reaction of oxygen species evolved from the structure with the electrolyte at the electrode surface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0090" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0090_R_d136764921e1471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37">37</a></sup></span> Notably, the absence of CO<sub>2</sub> evolution at high voltage in the O3/27P2 compound further substantiates the absence of oxygen release from the material. Instead, the O3/27P2 electrode displays only small amount of CO<sub>2</sub> release right after the initiation of the charging process, with the onset and flux closely resembling the initial CO<sub>2</sub> peak observed in the pure O3 compound. This probably arises from carbonates contamination on the oxide surface, a phenomenon commonly observed in literature and also present in the P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compound at a lower flux (Figure S17).<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0093" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0093_R_d136764921e1490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">38</a></sup></span> The unexpected absence, or significant suppression, of oxygen release in the O3/27P2 compound, despite its substantial anionic-redox capacity, has been consistently observed and confirmed through our DEMS experiments. It offers a clear explanation for the remarkable improvement in terms of first-cycle Coulombic efficiency when compared to the pure O3 phase. Additionally, this finding also supports the enhanced kinetics and cycling stability of the O3/27P2 structure. The suppression of oxygen evolution reduces irreversible structural organizations in the activation charge, contributing to the overall improved electrochemical performances.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202411059-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202411059-sec-0006-title"> Structural Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>Next, we investigate the structural origin of the suppression of gas release and the improvement of overall electrochemical performances using operando XRD (Figure S18–20). Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0005">5a</a> and Figure S18 show the evolution of the XRD patterns of O3/27P2 over the first two charge–discharge cycles at a current rate of C/10 within the voltage range of 1.5 to 4.5 V. Similar to the pure O3 phase (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0005">5b</a> and Figure S19) that undergoes a transition to another O3 stacking with (003) peaks at lower angles upon charge,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> the O3 component in the O3/27P2 compound also shows increased intensity at <i>d</i>-spacings of ~5.4 Å (Figure S21). The possibility of having O3-P3 phase transition is ruled out as the peak intensity of (104)<sub>O3</sub> remains dominant over (015)<sub>O3</sub> even at intermediate Na stoichiometry nearing the end of charge plateau (Figure S22), which is the characteristic of O3 structures (<i>R</i>-3<i>m</i> space group) rather than P3 structures (<i>R</i>3<i>m</i> space group). In the pure O3 compound, the absence of O3-P3 transition can be attributed to the Li migration into Na layers, which inhibits Na-vacancy ordering that leads to O3-P3 phase transition, which we assume can also be the case for O3/27P2.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> Therefore, we follow the nomenclature of the pure O3 compound and refer to the new phase as O3(II). It is noteworthy that this O3(I)-O3(II) transition component in the O3/27P2 is characterized by much more pronounced peak broadening (see contour plot in Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0005">5a</a>) compared to the clear biphasic process of the pure O3 phase.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e1533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> The smoother transition through peak broadening suggests a variety of close <i>d</i>-spacings in the O3 component, likely influenced by different conjunctions with the P2 component. Another major difference nests in the extended duration of this transition in the O3/27P2 material, as demonstrated by the prolonged preservation of (003)<sub>O3</sub> and (104)<sub>O3</sub> peak intensities.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202411059-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0a69123f-50e0-4550-b5f6-30fed9fb6ee0/anie202411059-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0a69123f-50e0-4550-b5f6-30fed9fb6ee0/anie202411059-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/53cc402d-c1e6-427f-9256-60549f640acb/anie202411059-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0a69123f-50e0-4550-b5f6-30fed9fb6ee0/anie202411059-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411059-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411059" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structural evolution of O3/27P2 and O3 upon cycling. The operando XRD patterns of the first 2 cycles (contour maps) and corresponding voltage profile at C/10 of (a) O3/27P2 and (b) O3.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Simultaneously, the (002) peak of the P2 component gradually shifts toward lower angles, reflecting an increased interlayer distance due to desodiation-induced increase in TMO<sub>2</sub> layer repulsion (Figure S20). This implies the involvement of the P2 structure in desodiation process right from the onset of the charge plateau at 3.8 V, unlike what we have suspected from its similarity in redox peaks to the pure P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> phase above 4 V (Figure S13).</p>
            
            <p>When charged above 4 V, the pure O3 phase undergoes a complex change characterized by (<i>hkl</i>)-dependent peak broadening and asymmetry. This transformation aligns with the onset of oxygen release (Figure <a href="#anie202411059-fig-0004">4e</a>), and leads to another O3(III) structure at the end of charge with (003) peak sharpness partially restored at ~18.5° 2<i>θ</i>. This end-of-charge O3(III) structure was previously characterized by synchrotron XRD, neutron diffraction and TEM.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0022_R_d136764921e1587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> Despite the absence of oxygen release, the O3 component in the O3/27P2 material exhibits a similar trend, albeit with a small portion of (003) peak intensity remaining at ~16.5° 2<i>θ</i>. Although the possibility of electrode inhomogeneity due to reduced stacking pressure in the operando cell cannot be ruled out, this phenomenon may also suggest the suppressed structural transitions of the O3 component, likely constrained by the phase boundary with P2 domains.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411059-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411059-bib-0044_R_d136764921e1593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18h - Angew. Chem.">18h</a></sup></span> This observation aligns with the restrained gas release and the improvement in cycling stability in the material with P2 intergrowth.</p>
            
            <p>On the other hand, the (002) peak of the P2 component undergoes a slight shift toward higher angles at the end of charge, accompanied by a reduction in intensity. Nevertheless, the intensity of the (102) peak is well preserved. Notably, no clear sign of the (002) peak belonging to the O2 stacking nor (004) of the OP4 stacking has been observed. HRTEM of the end-of-charge sample also revealed distinct O3 and P2 domains with no sign of O2 components (Figure S23).</p>
            
            <p>Upon discharge, the pure O3 phase does not completely revert to the pristine structure, displaying increased peak broadness and lowered peak intensity, suggesting structural irreversibility probably related to oxygen release. In contrast, the O3 component in the intergrowth structure exhibits significantly better restored peak intensity, which is well preserved throughout the second cycle. However, the reflections of the P2 component experiences a notable peak broadening and intensity decrease. In the end-of-discharge pattern, the shift of the (002) peak toward higher 2<i>θ</i> values than the pristine one suggests the additional Na<sup>+</sup> insertion accompanying Mn reduction, as has been observed in pure P2 phase (Figure S20).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411059-sec-0007">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411059-sec-0007-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we successfully controlled the P2 intergrowth ratio in O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by fine-tuning synthesis conditions, resulting in three novel O3/P2 intergrowth materials with the same overall composition. This allowed us to isolate the role of P2 intergrowth in anionic redox reversibility and kinetics from compositional changes. Through a combination of XPS, XAS, and EPR techniques, we confirmed the exclusive contribution of oxygen oxidation to the first charge capacity, reaching a record-high 260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, DEMS revealed significant suppression of irreversible oxygen release in the O3/P2 intergrowth material compared to the pure O3 material, and operando XRD demonstrated improved structural reversibility accordingly. This collectively results in an outstanding initial cycle Coulombic efficiency of 87 %, comparable to the best-performing Li-rich NMCs. Overall, the newly synthesized O3/27P2 intergrowth material, based on environmentally benign Mn, exhibits a reversible capacity of 226 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at C/20 and 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1C with excellent cycling stability, surpassing that of O3 and P2 phase pure compounds based on anionic redox. Our work demonstrates a promising strategy namely controlled P2 intergrowth in O3-Na<sub>1</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> for tailoring Na layered cathodes using highly reversible anionic redox reactions, holding significant potential for both high-capacity and high-rate applications.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
